        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="49a1eeb5dc7f4736b15e6fcc313d171c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '5d88b373-088e-4bad-8839-19c5514b0b96'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d15490922" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>To achieve the high-power sodium-ion batteries, the solid-state ion diffusion in the electrode materials is a highly concerned issue and needs to be solved. In this study, a simple and effective strategy is reported to weaken and degrade this process by engineering the intensified surface and near-surface reactions, which is realized by making use of a sandwich-type nanoarchitecture composed of graphene as electron channels and few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing. The unique 2D sheet-shaped hierarchical structure is capable of shortening the ion diffusion length, while the few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing has more accessible surface area and the decreased ion diffusion resistance, evidenced by the smaller energy barriers revealed by the density functional theory calculations. Benefiting from the shortened ion diffusion distance and enhanced electron transfer capability, a high ratio of surface or near-surface reactions is dominated at a high discharge/charge rate. As such, the composites exhibit the high capacities of 152 and 93 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 and 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, a high reversible capacity of 684 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and an excellent cycling stability up to 4500 cycles can be delivered. The outstanding performance is attributed to the engineered structure with increased contribution of surface or near-surface reactions.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201702486-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Electrochemical energy storage with both high energy density and high power density is highly demanded for powering the ever-growing fast-charging portable electric devices (PEDs), electric vehicles (EVs) and smart grids.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0001_R_d15490914e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Small Methods">1</a></span> Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have been extensively researched and widely used in the field of PEDs.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0002_R_d15490914e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></span> However, the large-scale applications of LIBs in diverse EVs, and the current and near-future smart grids powered by the renewable energy sources such as solar and wind energy are still restricted by the low power density of the devices, and the limited abundance of lithium resources with uneven distribution.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0003_R_d15490914e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Rev. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Sci.">3</a></span> Because of this, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn extensive attention as a potential and promising alternative to LIBs for large-scale applications due to the huge advantages of sodium in terms of low cost and large resource availability.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0004_R_d15490914e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces Acc. Chem. Res. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></span> However, some big challenges for SIBs need to be addressed, which suffer from more serious problems than LIBs in terms of the achievement of high energy density with high power density. This is because of the large resistance involved in the sluggish kinetics process due to the slow ion transport and electron transfer, particularly, at a high discharge/charge rate that are rooted primarily from larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0005_R_d15490914e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Nanotechnol. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></span> The slow ion transport and electron transfer may not only give rise to a large energy loss, but also lead to huge heat generation which may further result in serious safety problems, which is the case in particular for large devices.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0006_R_d15490914e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Curr. Opin. Solid State Mater. Sci.">6</a></span> As such, it is significant and challenging to enhance the kinetics of SIBs reactions, i.e., to accelerate the mass transport and electron transfer rate in SIBs.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0007_R_d15490914e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Energy">7</a></span></p>
         
         <p>One of the effective strategies to address these bottleneck problems is to tune the reversible reactions occurring on the surface or near-surface of the electrode materials, which can be done by shortening the ion diffusion length and electron transport distance, i.e., to enable the reactions being finished in a short time, which has been proved in electrochemical capacitors with similar electrochemical nature.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0008_R_d15490914e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">8</a></span> It is known that the surface reaction contribution is closely related to the morphology and particle size of the electrode materials. Dunn and co-workers have quantitatively evaluated the impact of the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> particle size on the surface reaction contribution, and confirmed the contribution of surface or near-surface reactions. Such a surface reaction could account for more than 50% of the total reaction when the average particle size is smaller than 10 nm.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0009_R_d15490914e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Phys. Chem. C">9</a></span> This leads one to envision that it is possible to further promote the reaction kinetics by synthesizing electrode materials with unique morphology and tuned nanoscale dimensions and increased surface area, which will help to improve the solid-state ion diffusion.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0010_R_d15490914e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun. Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></span> To fabricate SIBs with both high power and energy density, anode materials with rational structure are indispensable, in which the contribution of the surface or near surface reactions can be tuned to some extent.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report on the design and synthesis of sandwich-type nanoarchitecture in which the MoS<sub>2</sub> is strongly coupled on graphene nanosheets (G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C) via in situ complexation and polymerization reactions guided by graphene that functioned as the structure direction agent, followed by high-temperature gas sulfuration treatment. The strong complexation between Mo ions and polydopamine (PDA) favors the formation of few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing. The carbon species derived from PDA (C-PDA) serves as a binder to strongly couple MoS<sub>2</sub> with the graphene nanosheets. The 2D sheet-shaped structure is capable of shortening the ion diffusion length, while the few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing has more accessible surface area of active materials and the decreased ion diffusion resistance, evidenced by density functional theory (DFT) study, showing that the energy barriers of Na ion diffusion in-between the layers of the MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing can be decreased, indicative of the improved reaction kinetics. The graphene intercalated in the hybrid G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C acts as both the channels for fast electron conduction and the substrate for rapid surface reactions. Due to the combined effects mentioned above, the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C has shown a significant superiority in SIBs with high rate capability, evidenced by the high capacity of 152 and 93 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at an ultrahigh current density of 30 and 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Meanwhile, it also exhibits a high reversible capacity of 684 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent cycling stability up to 4500 cycles. The outstanding electrochemical performance of the hybrid G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C is believed to be due to the unique engineered structure with increased contribution of surface or near-surface reactions.</p>
         
         <p>The synthetic strategy of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets involving in situ complexation and polymerization reactions followed by high-temperature gas sulfuration step is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0001">1</a>a. Mo ions are firstly coupled with the dopamine via complexation reactions. Then, a fast in situ polymerization reaction of dopamine takes place on the surface of GO nanosheets under alkaline conditions. After that, the gas sulfuration is conducted for the formation of high-crystalline MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702486-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/61610d20-d981-48fe-a41d-399699f19846/adma201702486-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/61610d20-d981-48fe-a41d-399699f19846/adma201702486-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fbd527e1-e152-40a9-8bb0-382284858410/adma201702486-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/61610d20-d981-48fe-a41d-399699f19846/adma201702486-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702486-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702486" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic for the formation of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets. b–d) SEM images, e–f) TEM images, and g) HRTEM image of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The representative scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets (Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0001">1</a>b,d) show that the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C sample has a sheet-shaped structure with a thickness of ≈130 nm. The magnified SEM image (Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0001">1</a>c) shows that the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets are made of many small nanoparticles. For comparison, the pure MoS<sub>2</sub>-C composites were also prepared in the absence of graphene template, of which the SEM and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images are shown in Figure S1 in the Supporting Information. It can be seen that the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C composites feature a typical sphere-shaped structure with a diameter of ≈400 nm. Similarly, the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres are also built of many small nanoparticles. With this information, one may have confidence to believe that the GO nanosheets function as the structure-directed agent, and are responsible for the formation of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets with shortened ion diffusion length for electrodes in SIBs.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0011_R_d15490914e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span> In the hybrid G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets for SIBs, the graphene has another role as the electron conductive channels to ensure rapid electron transfer in the composites.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0012_R_d15490914e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Lett. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></span> The sheet-shaped structure of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C resulted from the graphene-directed growth is further confirmed by the TEM examination, of which the typical images are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0001">1</a>e,f. Specifically, the graphene nanosheets are encapsulated by nanoparticles, yielding a typical sandwich-type structure. The high resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets reveals that these nanoparticles are made of the MoS<sub>2</sub> and C-PDA (Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0001">1</a>g). It is assumed here that the C-PDA functions as a bridge or glue to strongly bind MoS<sub>2</sub> onto the surface of the graphene nanosheets. The energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometer elemental mapping also indicates that the MoS<sub>2</sub> is uniformly distributed in the nanosheets (Figure S2, Supporting Information). Interestingly, the MoS<sub>2</sub> in the sandwich-type architecture shows a few-layered feature (≤3 layers), with a lattice spacing of ≈0.73 nm corresponding to the (002) crystal plane of MoS<sub>2</sub> phase, indicative of an expanded interlayer spacing.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0013_R_d15490914e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Sci. Rep. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></span> These features of MoS<sub>2</sub> can be attributed to the strong coordination between Mo ions and PDA, leading to the confined growth of MoS<sub>2</sub> at high temperature, which is also evidenced by the HRTEM image of MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres (Figure S1d, Supporting Information). The porous structure of the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets and MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres was analyzed by nitrogen adsorption technique. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0002">2</a>a, the isotherms for G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets are typical type IV, indicative of the balanced pore structure consisting of mesopores and macropores. Moreover, the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets have a Brunauer–Emmett–Teller specific surface area of 126 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, higher than 78 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres. The increased surface area is desired for the fast and enhanced adsorption of Na ions in SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702486-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8151936b-aa03-4c66-98ec-930cf105f8e9/adma201702486-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8151936b-aa03-4c66-98ec-930cf105f8e9/adma201702486-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff569786-7746-4fd7-ad96-0879b1c3014f/adma201702486-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8151936b-aa03-4c66-98ec-930cf105f8e9/adma201702486-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702486-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702486" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and the pore-size distribution (inset) of MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets. b) XRD patterns and c) Raman spectra of the commercial MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres, and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets. d) Mo K-edge Fourier-transformed EXAFS of the commercial MoS<sub>2</sub> and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The structure, composition and surface chemical states of the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets were further examined by X-ray diffraction (XRD) technique, Raman spectroscopy, and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). From the XRD patterns of MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0002">2</a>b, it is interesting to note that the MoS<sub>2</sub> (002) characteristic peak is missing in both cases, evidencing that the MoS<sub>2</sub> in G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets and MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres features a few-layered structure, which is also consistent with the TEM results.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0014_R_d15490914e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">14</a></span> Moreover, the characteristic peaks of (100) and (110) crystal planes at 32.8° and 58.4° indicate the formation of the hexagonal layered MoS<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0015_R_d15490914e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eur. J.">15</a></span> This is further confirmed by the Raman results shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0002">2</a>c. The characteristic peaks centering at 380.1 and 403.2 cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> modes of MoS<sub>2</sub> with a typical hexagonal layered structure, respectively.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0016_R_d15490914e755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy Adv. Mater. Carbon">16</a></span> The E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> is related to the in-plane vibration of Mo and sulfur atoms, while A<sub>1g</sub> represents the out-of-plane vibration of sulfur atoms. Compared with the commercial MoS<sub>2</sub>, the wave number of A<sub>1g</sub> characteristic peak has shifted from 408.2 cm<sup>−1</sup> of commercial MoS<sub>2</sub> to 403.2 cm<sup>−1</sup> of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets. This obvious shift is mainly attributed to the features of few-layered structure and the expanded interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> giving rise to a diminished interlayer van der Waals force which would result in a stronger out-of-plane vibration.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0017_R_d15490914e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></span> The characteristic D-band and G-band peaks indicate the presence of C-PDA and graphene. The extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectrum of the commercial MoS<sub>2</sub> reveals two apparent characteristic peaks corresponding to the Mo<span class="icomoon"></span>S and Mo<span class="icomoon"></span>Mo bond, respectively, which are due to the presence of the nearest neighboring sulfur atoms and Mo atoms around a central Mo atom (Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0002">2</a>d). The EXAFS spectrum of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets shows a well-developed Mo<span class="icomoon"></span>S peak but relatively weak Mo<span class="icomoon"></span>Mo peak, which may be due to the few-layered structure and the expanded interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> in hybrid G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0018_R_d15490914e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Sci. Rep.">18</a></span> The typical XPS survey spectrum reveals that the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets mainly consist of Mo, S, C, and O elements (Figure S3a, Supporting Information). The two peaks centering at 284.6 and 285.5 eV in the high-resolution C 1s XPS spectra (Figure S3b, Supporting Information) correspond to C<span class="icomoon"></span>C and C<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond, respectively. The Mo 3d and S 2p XPS spectra shown in Figure S3c,d in the Supporting Information exhibit typical MoS<sub>2</sub> features. The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) reveals that the ratio of the MoS<sub>2</sub>, C-PDA and graphene in hybrid G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C are 41.8, 53.0, and 5.2%, respectively (Figure S4, Supporting Information). These results mentioned above have demonstrated that the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets with a sandwich-type structure have been successfully synthesized. The unique morphology and the engineered structure and chemical components of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets endow it with outstanding ability for the enhanced contribution of surface or near-surface reactions when employed as the anode materials for SIBs. To be specific, the 2D sheet-shaped structure is capable of shortening the ion diffusion length, and the few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing enables the increase of the accessible surface area of active materials and the decrease of the ion diffusion resistance, which are further confirmed by the DFT calculations. The graphene intercalated in the hybrid G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C acts as the electron conductive channels, which helps to ensure the rapid surface reactions. The strong intimate interaction between MoS<sub>2</sub> and C-PDA helps to effectively prevent the detachment of MoS<sub>2</sub> from the graphene sheets, and facilitate the fast electron transfer during the discharge/charge process.</p>
         
         <p>To explore the potential of the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets as anode materials for SIBs, the electrochemical performance was evaluated by using coin-type cells in a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were carried out to analyze the electrochemical reaction mechanism of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets in SIBs, of which the corresponding curves are shown in Figure S5 in the Supporting Information. In the first cycle, the reduction peaks at 1.05 and 0.82 V correspond to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into MoS<sub>2</sub> and the formation of the solid electrolyte interface layer on the surface of the electrode materials.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0019_R_d15490914e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small">19</a></span> The peak near 0.01 V is assigned to the conversion reaction from MoS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S and metallic Mo as well as the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into carbon interlayers. In addition, only one broad oxidation peak is observed at 1.9 V, which is associated with the oxidation of the metallic Mo to MoS<sub>2</sub>. The CV curves almost overlap from second to fifth cycle, indicative of the outstanding reversibility of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C electrode.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C electrode was further investigated by galvanostatic discharge–charge measurements. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0003">3</a>a shows the discharge–charge profiles in the first three cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Surprisingly, a high initial discharge capacity of 1095 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is delivered, with a high reversible charge capacity of 684 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, indicative of a high material utilization, implying the enough reaction kinetics that is due to the combined effects of the unique structure and the engineered components. The almost overlapped discharge–charge profiles in the second and third cycle indicate an excellent reversibility. The discharge–charge capacity and reversibility of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets were further investigated at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, of which the results are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0003">3</a>b and Figure S6 in the Supporting Information. The G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets have a high reversible capacity of 451 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to a high capacity retention ratio of 76% after 400 cycles. In contrast, the as-made MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres have a low reversible capacity, only 98 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 400 cycles. To further explore the cycling stability of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets, the long-term cycle performance measurement at a large current density of 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> was performed, of which the results are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0003">3</a>c. Even after 4500 cycles, a high reversible capacity of 253 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be kept, corresponding to a low cyclic fading rate of 0.01% per cycle. The excellent cycling stability is attributed to the unique sheet-shaped structure of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C composites and the few-layered structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> for accommodating the large volume change and the strongly coupled effects of C-PDA for preventing the detachment of MoS<sub>2</sub> from the graphene substrate. This can be further confirmed by the unchanged structure of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C after 100 cycles (Figure S7, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702486-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f24341e1-ac2c-4d82-a059-30406f03b889/adma201702486-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f24341e1-ac2c-4d82-a059-30406f03b889/adma201702486-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/72aa85e8-731c-4a94-a134-62c6f1756b74/adma201702486-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f24341e1-ac2c-4d82-a059-30406f03b889/adma201702486-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702486-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702486" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Discharge–charge profiles of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C electrode for the first three cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Cycle performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) The long-term cycle performance of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets at a large current density of 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Rate performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets at different densities from 0.1 to 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) A comparison of the rate performance between this work and the work previously reported in literature.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To figure out the surface and near-surface reaction behaviors of the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets in SIBs, the rate capability of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets was tested at different current densities ranging from 0.1 to 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>, of which the results are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0003">3</a>d and Figure S8 in the Supporting Information. It is notable that with an increase of the current density, the capacities delivered by the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres electrode drop sharply. While in the case of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets, the high capacities of 654, 597, 531, 480, 361, 287, and 201 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be delivered at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 5, 10, and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at the ultrahigh current densities of 30 and 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets still achieve high reversible capacities of 152 and 93 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are much higher than those of the as-made MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres (0.78 and 0.45 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) under the same conditions. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0004">4</a>a, the capacity retention rate (the ratio of the discharge capacities at different current densities to the discharge capacity at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) of MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres is sensitive to the current density, and quickly drops with an increase of the current density. In sharp contrast, the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets still can keep high capacity retention rates of 23% and 14% even with 300 and 500-fold increase of current density. The high capacity retention rate for the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets would mainly be attributed to surface or near-surface reactions derived from their unique morphology and structure. The possible reaction process involved in electrochemical process is schemed in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0004">4</a>b. With the increase of the discharge/charge rate, the electrochemical reactions more easily occur on the surface of electrode materials instead of bulk phase due to the requirements of fast mass transport and electron transfer. As such, the surface and near-surface reaction behaviors are dominated at a high current density.[<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0004_R_d15490914e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces Acc. Chem. Res. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma201702486-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0020_R_d15490914e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></span>] In the case of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C, the 2D sheet-shaped structure of composites and the few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing enable the increase of the accessible surface area of active materials and the decrease of the ion diffusion resistance, thus resulting in the high-efficiency utilization of the active materials. The intercalated graphene acts as the electron conductive channels, which is another key factor for the rapid reactions. These features are combined together to make a contribution to the high surface and near-surface reactions even at a large discharge/charge rate, resulting in the high utilization of active materials. However, for the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres, the story is totally different, evidenced by a low material utilization at a large discharge/charge rate, which is mainly limited by the low surface area and electronic conductivity though the few-layer MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing is also present. Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0003">3</a>e presents the comparison of the rate capability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials in SIBs between this work and previous work in the literature. To the best of our knowledge, the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets exhibit the best rate capability among the MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials reported for SIBs thus far.[<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0011_R_d15490914e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#adma201702486-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0013_R_d15490914e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Sci. Rep. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a>, <a href="#adma201702486-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0015_R_d15490914e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eur. J.">15</a>, <a href="#adma201702486-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0016_R_d15490914e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy Adv. Mater. Carbon">16</a>, <a href="#adma201702486-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0021_R_d15490914e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Electrochim. Acta Sci. Rep. ACS Nano">21</a></span>]</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702486-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/195383a3-396a-4163-b4be-b80a0dcb96c2/adma201702486-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/195383a3-396a-4163-b4be-b80a0dcb96c2/adma201702486-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/42db6b51-40d4-4c15-93a2-636f895647fe/adma201702486-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/195383a3-396a-4163-b4be-b80a0dcb96c2/adma201702486-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702486-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702486" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The capacity retention rates of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets and MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres at different current densities. b) Schematic for the electrochemical reactions involved in the hybrid G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets and MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres at different discharge/charge rates.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The surface and near-surface reaction behaviors of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets can be further verified by the electrochemical CV curves and the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0005">5</a>a shows the CV curves of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets measured at different scan rates. It can be seen that the CV curves of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets can keep a similar shape as the scan rate increases, indicative of the excellent adaptability at large scan rates. Compared with that of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets, the CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres at large scan rates show an obvious deformation, indicative of the serious polarizations due to the sluggish reaction kinetics (Figure S9, Supporting Information). Based on the equation of <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, the contributions of the surface reactions at different voltages can be evaluated. <i>b</i> is a value ranging from 0.5 to 1, representing the reactions from bulk to the surface or near-surface, respectively.[<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0004_R_d15490914e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces Acc. Chem. Res. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma201702486-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0022_R_d15490914e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Mater. J. Phys. Chem. C Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></span>] As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0005">5</a>b, at the peak A and peak B (shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0005">5</a>a) of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets, the <i>b</i> value is as high as 0.92 and 0.82, respectively, suggesting the high ratio of the contribution from surface reactions. Moreover, the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets show higher contribution of surface reactions than that of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres at a wide voltage range (Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0005">5</a>c), which is attributed to the unique sheet-shaped structure and the high conductivity of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets. The EIS measurements of the as-made G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets and MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres were performed at open circuit voltage within a frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz. From the Nyquist plots shown in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0005">5</a>d, it can be worked out that the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C electrode can deliver a smaller diameter of the semicircle in the high-medium frequency region, indicative of smaller charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and higher electrical conductivity than that of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres electrode.<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0023_R_d15490914e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></span> This is attributed to the rapid electron transfer channel effects of graphene in the sandwich-type structure. In the low frequency region of the Nyquist plots, the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C electrode also presents a larger slope than the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres, corresponding to a smaller Warburg impedance. This indicates a faster solid-state ion diffusion in the bulk electrode of G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C composites because the unique sheet-shaped structure can decrease the ion diffusion length.[<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0004_R_d15490914e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces Acc. Chem. Res. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma201702486-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0024_R_d15490914e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">24</a></span>]</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702486-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/43d481cc-b330-40d7-89d1-c4394ceb92e9/adma201702486-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/43d481cc-b330-40d7-89d1-c4394ceb92e9/adma201702486-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/99ced024-9557-484c-a8ec-2fcf2b958ce0/adma201702486-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/43d481cc-b330-40d7-89d1-c4394ceb92e9/adma201702486-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702486-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702486" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets at different scan rates. b) Current response vs. the scan rate of the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets at the voltages of peak A and peak B. c) <i>b</i> value of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets at different voltages. d) Nyquist plots of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanospheres and G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To have a further insight into the positive effects of MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing on mitigating diffusion resistance in SIBs, the DFT calculations were conducted by the Vienna ab initio simulation package,<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0025_R_d15490914e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Power Sources">25</a></span> and the results are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0006">6</a>. To simulate the diffusion process of a Na atom in MoS<sub>2</sub>, we considered bulk 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> in AB stacking with 4 × 4 × 1 unit cells. The Brillouin zone of the supercell was sampled by 3 × 3 × 3 uniform k-point mesh. The climbing-image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method was employed to determine the transition state and activation energy for a Na atom to migrate from one position in-between the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers to another (Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0006">6</a>a,b). Twelve diffusion sites were used to mimic the diffusion path. The initial (A, diffusion site 0) and final states (E, diffusion site 11) were optimized by only ionic and electronic degrees of freedom using thresholds for the total energy of 10<sup>−4</sup> eV and force of 0.02 eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. The diffusion barriers at different transition states in-between the layers of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> with different interlayer spacings of 6.17, 6.60, and 7.00 Å are presented in Figure <a href="#adma201702486-fig-0006">6</a>c. It can be noted that the highest diffusion energy barrier is delivered in 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> with the smallest interlayer spacing up to 1.30 eV, which is much higher than that in 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing of 6.60 Å (0.70 eV) and 7.00 Å (0.30 eV), indicating that the expanded interlayer spacing helps to obviously decrease the diffusion resistance. In view of the phase transition from 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> to 1T-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoS<sub>2</sub> during Na ions intercalation process,<span><a href="#adma201702486-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201702486-bib-0025_R_d15490914e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Power Sources">25</a></span> the diffusibility of Na ions in 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> with different expanded interlayer spacings was also investigated. As shown in Figure S10 in the Supporting Information, the decreased diffusion energy barriers are demonstrated with the increase of the 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayer spacing. These combined characteristics further demonstrate that the expanded interlayer spacing is in favor of intensifying the mass transportation in SIBs process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201702486-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ce26ea00-a427-4a6d-af10-615196127550/adma201702486-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ce26ea00-a427-4a6d-af10-615196127550/adma201702486-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/290a43c7-6229-4413-92ef-935c43e7fff5/adma201702486-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ce26ea00-a427-4a6d-af10-615196127550/adma201702486-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201702486-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201702486" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Schematic illustration for the diffusion process of a Na atom in-between the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers with different interlayer spacings of 6.17, 6.60, and 7.00 Å, respectively. c) The curves of diffusion energy barriers at different transition states: the MoS<sub>2</sub> with different interlayer spacing of 6.17 (black), 6.60 (red), and 7.00 Å (green).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, the sandwich-type G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets have been designed and successfully prepared by a novel two-step strategy involving the in situ complexation and polymerization reactions with graphene as the structure-directing agent and the high-temperature gas sulfuration. The 2D sheet-shaped structure helps to shorten the solid-ion diffusion length, the few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> with expanded interlayer spacing can increase the available and accessible active area, and decrease the ion diffusion resistance, evidenced by the smaller energy barriers revealed by the DFT calculations. The graphene in the sandwich-type structure as the electron channels helps to enhance electrical conductivity of the whole composites. Benefiting from these structure merits, the G@MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanosheets show ultrahigh surface or near-surface reaction contributions, evidenced by the high rate capacity of 93 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The present work has demonstrated a novel strategy to configure the MoS<sub>2</sub> composites with unique structure, and may open a new way for design of high-rate anode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
